                                        <path fill-rule="evenodd" clip-rule="evenodd" fill="#FFFFFF" d="M45.201,12.343c0.378,0.48,0.758,0.925,1.096,1.401    c2.975,4.207,4.543,8.876,4.494,14.044c-0.05,5.452-1.643,10.386-5.186,14.593c-3.484,4.133-7.929,6.73-13.182,7.895    c-6.313,1.398-12.216,0.275-17.695-3.131c-0.441-0.273-0.847-0.6-1.266-0.904c-0.11-0.078-0.208-0.174-0.337-0.287    c0.127-0.141,0.246-0.27,0.366-0.398c0.887-0.949,1.765-1.904,2.663-2.844c0.114-0.119,0.321-0.217,0.485-0.217    c3.658-0.006,7.318,0,10.975,0.008c3.458,0.006,6.913,0.02,10.369,0.02c0.957,0,1.871-0.193,2.62-0.844    c0.797-0.693,1.157-1.596,1.157-2.643c0.001-7.533,0.003-15.067-0.005-22.601c-0.002-0.309,0.088-0.524,0.3-0.743    C43.098,14.598,44.127,13.49,45.201,12.343"></path>
                                        <path fill-rule="evenodd" clip-rule="evenodd" fill="#FFFFFF" d="M41.402,8.822c-0.99,1.027-1.994,2.021-2.935,3.072    c-0.312,0.35-0.616,0.416-1.036,0.415c-6.98-0.009-13.957-0.007-20.938-0.007c-2.039,0-3.561,1.514-3.561,3.557    c0,6.504,0.002,13.008,0.006,19.512c0.002,0.973,0.011,1.943,0.004,2.914c0,0.133-0.04,0.301-0.127,0.393    c-1.069,1.162-2.15,2.314-3.229,3.469c-0.021,0.023-0.052,0.039-0.109,0.08c-0.159-0.188-0.323-0.369-0.471-0.562    c-2.535-3.348-4.119-7.102-4.605-11.268c-0.61-5.229,0.194-10.229,2.835-14.839c2.669-4.664,6.655-7.805,11.618-9.75    c3.205-1.257,6.533-1.852,9.977-1.621c4.478,0.298,8.553,1.754,12.227,4.325c0.101,0.072,0.197,0.151,0.291,0.229    C41.364,8.755,41.374,8.778,41.402,8.822"></path>
                                        <path fill-rule="evenodd" clip-rule="evenodd" fill="#FFFFFF" d="M39.799,12.47c0.873-0.911,1.749-1.829,2.676-2.797    c0.605,0.564,1.195,1.112,1.816,1.691c-0.941,0.985-1.817,1.903-2.703,2.83c-0.276-0.339-0.511-0.688-0.807-0.975    C40.492,12.941,40.145,12.728,39.799,12.47"></path>

<article class="post tag-threat-spotlight tag-cisco-talos-antivirus tag-cisco-talos-network-intrusion-prevention tag-cisco-talos-web-filtering tag-cisco-talos-dns-security featured ">
        <div class="feature-image-wrapper my-5">
            <figure>
                <img src="/content/images/2026/02/DKnife-header--1-.jpg" alt="" class="img-fluid" />

    <h1 class="text-center">Knife Cutting the Edge: Disclosing a China-nexus gateway-monitoring AitM framework</h1>

    <div class="text-center m-1">
        <div class="post-author">
            <span>By </span>
                <a href="https://blog.talosintelligence.com/author/ashley/">Ashley Shen</a>
        </div>

        <br/>
        <time class="post-datetime" datetime="February 5, 2026 06:00">
            Thursday, February 5, 2026 06:00
        </time>

        <div class="m-3">
                            <a href="/category/threat-spotlight/" class="category primary-category">
                                Threat Spotlight
                            </a>
                        
        </div>
    </div>

    <section class="post-content-wrapper mt-5">
        <div class="post-content" id="post-content">
            <ul><li>Cisco Talos&nbsp;uncovered “DKnife,”&nbsp;a fully featured&nbsp;gateway-monitoring and&nbsp;adversary-in-the-middle (AitM)&nbsp;framework&nbsp;comprising seven Linux-based implants that perform deep-packet inspection, manipulate traffic, and deliver malware via routers and edge devices.&nbsp;Based on the artifact metadata,&nbsp;DKnife&nbsp;has been used since at least 2019 and the&nbsp;command and control (C2)&nbsp;are&nbsp;still active as of&nbsp;January&nbsp;2026.</li><li>DKnife’s&nbsp;attacks target&nbsp;a wide range of devices, including PCs, mobile devices, and&nbsp;Internet of Things (IoT)&nbsp;devices. It delivers and interacts with the&nbsp;<a href="https://malpedia.caad.fkie.fraunhofer.de/details/win.shadowpad" rel="noreferrer noopener"><u>ShadowPad</u></a>&nbsp;and&nbsp;<a href="https://www.trendmicro.com/en_us/research/24/l/earth-minotaur.html" rel="noreferrer noopener"><u>DarkNimbus</u></a>&nbsp;backdoors by&nbsp;hijacking&nbsp;binary downloads and Android application updates.</li><li>DKnife&nbsp;primarily targets Chinese-speaking users, indicated by credential harvesting for Chinese-language services, exfiltration modules for popular Chinese&nbsp;mobile&nbsp;applications&nbsp;and code references to Chinese media domains. Based on&nbsp;the language used in the&nbsp;code, configuration&nbsp;files&nbsp;and the&nbsp;<a href="https://blog.talosintelligence.com/chinese-hacking-group-apt41-compromised-taiwanese-government-affiliated-research-institute-with-shadowpad-and-cobaltstrike-2/" rel="noreferrer noopener"><u>ShadowPad</u></a>&nbsp;malware delivered in the campaign,&nbsp;we assess with high confidence that&nbsp;China-nexus&nbsp;threat actors&nbsp;operate this tool.</li><li>We discovered&nbsp;a link between&nbsp;DKnife&nbsp;and a campaign delivering&nbsp;<a href="https://www.welivesecurity.com/en/eset-research/thewizards-apt-group-slaac-spoofing-adversary-in-the-middle-attacks/" rel="noreferrer noopener"><u>WizardNet</u></a><u>,</u>&nbsp;a modular backdoor known to be delivered by a different&nbsp;AiTM&nbsp;framework&nbsp;<a href="https://www.welivesecurity.com/en/eset-research/thewizards-apt-group-slaac-spoofing-adversary-in-the-middle-attacks/" rel="noreferrer noopener"><u>Spellbinder</u></a>,&nbsp;suggesting&nbsp;a shared development or operational lineage.</li></ul><hr><h2 id="background">Background&nbsp;</h2><p>Since 2023, Cisco Talos has&nbsp;continuously tracked the&nbsp;<a href="https://citizenlab.ca/2019/09/poison-carp-tibetan-groups-targeted-with-1-click-mobile-exploits/" rel="noreferrer noopener"><u>MOONSHINE</u></a>&nbsp;exploit kit and the&nbsp;<a href="https://www.trendmicro.com/en_us/research/24/l/earth-minotaur.html" rel="noreferrer noopener"><u>DarkNimbus</u></a>&nbsp;backdoor&nbsp;it distributes. The exploit&nbsp;kit&nbsp;and backdoor were historically used for delivering Android and iOS exploits.&nbsp;While hunting for&nbsp;DarkNimbus&nbsp;samples,&nbsp;Talos&nbsp;discovered an&nbsp;executable and linkable format (ELF)&nbsp;binary communicating with the same&nbsp;C2&nbsp;server as the&nbsp;DarkNimbus&nbsp;backdoor, which retrieved a&nbsp;gzip-compressed archive. Analysis revealed that the archive&nbsp;contained&nbsp;a fully featured gateway monitoring and&nbsp;AiTM&nbsp;framework, dubbed “DKnife” by its developer.&nbsp;Based on the artifact metadata, the tool&nbsp;has been&nbsp;used since at least 2019, and the C2 is still active&nbsp;as of&nbsp;January 2026.&nbsp;</p><h2 id="link-between-dknife-and-wizardnet-campaigns">Link&nbsp;between&nbsp;DKnife&nbsp;and&nbsp;WizardNet&nbsp;campaigns&nbsp;</h2><p>During&nbsp;Talos'&nbsp;pivot on the&nbsp;C2&nbsp;infrastructure associated with&nbsp;DKnife, we&nbsp;identified&nbsp;additional&nbsp;servers&nbsp;exhibiting&nbsp;open ports and configurations consistent with previously observed&nbsp;DKnife&nbsp;deployments. Notably, one host (43.132.205[.]118)&nbsp;displayed port activity characteristic of&nbsp;DKnife&nbsp;infrastructure and was additionally found hosting the&nbsp;WizardNet&nbsp;backdoor on port 8881.&nbsp;</p><p>WizardNet&nbsp;is a modular backdoor first&nbsp;<a href="https://www.welivesecurity.com/en/eset-research/thewizards-apt-group-slaac-spoofing-adversary-in-the-middle-attacks/" rel="noreferrer noopener"><u>disclosed by ESET</u></a>&nbsp;in April 2025, known to be deployed via Spellbinder, a framework that performs&nbsp;AitM&nbsp;attacks leveraging IPv6 Stateless Address Autoconfiguration (SLAAC) spoofing.&nbsp;</p><p>Network responses from&nbsp;the&nbsp;WizardNet&nbsp;server&nbsp;align closely with the tactics, techniques, and procedures (TTPs) documented in ESET’s analysis. Specifically, the server delivered JSON-formatted tasking instructions that included a download URL pointing to an archive named&nbsp;<em>minibrowser11_rpl.zip</em>,&nbsp;which&nbsp;include the&nbsp;Wizardnet&nbsp;backdoor downloader.&nbsp;&nbsp;</p>
<!--kg-card-begin: html-->
<pre>{ 
  "CSoftID": 22, 
  "CommandLine": "", 
  "Desp": "1.1.1160.80", 
  "DownloadUrl": "http://43.132.205.118:81/app/minibrowser11_rpl.zip", 
  "ErrCode": 0, 
  "File": "minibrowser11.zip", 
  "Flags": 1, 
  "Hash": "cd09f8f7ea3b57d5eb6f3f16af445454", 
  "InstallType": 0, 
  "NewVer": "1.1.1160.900", 
  "PatchFile": "QBDeltaUpdate.exe", 
  "PatchHash": "cd09f8f7ea3b57d5eb6f3f16af445454", 
  "Sign": "", 
  "Size": 36673429, 
  "VerType": "" 
} </pre>
<!--kg-card-end: html-->
<p>Spellbinder’s TTPs, which involve hijacking legitimate application update requests and serving forged responses to redirect victims to malicious download URLs, are&nbsp;similar to&nbsp;DKnife’s&nbsp;method&nbsp;of&nbsp;compromising&nbsp;Android application updates.&nbsp;Spellbinder has also been&nbsp;observed&nbsp;distributing the&nbsp;DarkNimbus&nbsp;backdoor, whose&nbsp;C2&nbsp;infrastructure previously led to the&nbsp;initial&nbsp;discovery of&nbsp;DKnife. The URL redirection paths (<code>http[:]//[IP]:81/app/[app name]</code>)&nbsp;and port configurations&nbsp;identified&nbsp;in these cases are identical to those used by&nbsp;DKnife,&nbsp;indicating&nbsp;a shared development or operational lineage.&nbsp;&nbsp;</p><h2 id="targeting-scope">Targeting&nbsp;scope&nbsp;&nbsp;</h2><p>Based on artifacts recovered from the&nbsp;DKnife&nbsp;framework, this&nbsp;campaign&nbsp;appears to primarily target&nbsp;Chinese-speaking users. Indicators supporting this assessment include data collection and processing logic explicitly designed for Chinese&nbsp;mail&nbsp;services&nbsp;, as well as parsing and exfiltration modules tailored for Chinese mobile applications and messaging platforms, including WeChat. In addition, code references to Chinese media domains&nbsp;were&nbsp;identified&nbsp;in both the binaries and configuration files. The screenshot below illustrates an Android application hijacking response that targeted a Chinese&nbsp;taxi&nbsp;service&nbsp;and rideshare&nbsp;application.&nbsp;</p><p>It is important to note that&nbsp;Talos obtained&nbsp;the configuration files&nbsp;for analysis&nbsp;from a single&nbsp;C2&nbsp;server.&nbsp;Therefore, it&nbsp;remains&nbsp;possible that the operators employ different servers or configurations for distinct regional targeting scopes. Considering the connection between&nbsp;DKnife&nbsp;and the&nbsp;WizardNet&nbsp;campaign and&nbsp;given ESET’s reporting that&nbsp;WizardNet&nbsp;activity has targeted the Philippines, Cambodia, and the United Arab Emirates, we cannot rule out a broader regional or multilingual targeting scope.&nbsp;</p><figure class="kg-card kg-image-card kg-card-hascaption"><img src="https://blog.talosintelligence.com/content/images/2026/02/data-src-image-cd7880dc-0e1a-4387-a522-871ed8844138-1.png" class="kg-image" alt="" loading="lazy" width="500" height="419"><figcaption><i><em class="italic" style="white-space: pre-wrap;">Figure 1.</em></i> <i><em class="italic" style="white-space: pre-wrap;">The manifest response used for Android application update.</em></i></figcaption></figure><h2 id="indication-of-chinese-speaking-threat-actors">Indication of&nbsp;Chinese-speaking&nbsp;threat&nbsp;actors&nbsp;</h2><p>DKnife&nbsp;contains&nbsp;several artifacts that suggest the developer and operators are familiar with Simplified Chinese. Multiple comments written in Simplified Chinese appear throughout the&nbsp;DKnife&nbsp;configuration files (see&nbsp;Figure 2).&nbsp;</p><figure class="kg-card kg-image-card kg-card-hascaption"><img src="https://blog.talosintelligence.com/content/images/2026/02/data-src-image-27222d83-302e-423b-9e7c-7b4aefa5d41c.png" class="kg-image" alt="" loading="lazy" width="901" height="271" srcset="https://blog.talosintelligence.com/content/images/size/w600/2026/02/data-src-image-27222d83-302e-423b-9e7c-7b4aefa5d41c.png 600w, https://blog.talosintelligence.com/content/images/2026/02/data-src-image-27222d83-302e-423b-9e7c-7b4aefa5d41c.png 901w" sizes="(min-width: 720px) 720px"><figcaption><i><em class="italic" style="white-space: pre-wrap;">Figure 2.</em></i> <i><em class="italic" style="white-space: pre-wrap;">Example of Simplified Chinese language used in the comment of configuration files.</em></i></figcaption></figure><p>One&nbsp;component&nbsp;of&nbsp;DKnife&nbsp;is named&nbsp;yitiji.bin. The term "Yitiji" is the Pinyin (official romanization system for Mandarin Chinese) for "<a href="https://dictionary.hantrainerpro.com/chinese-english/translation-yitiji_allinonemach.htm" rel="noreferrer noopener"><u>一体机</u></a>" which means "all-in-one." In&nbsp;DKnife, this&nbsp;component&nbsp;is responsible for&nbsp;opening the local interface on the device to route traffic through a single device in this scenario.&nbsp;</p><p>Additionally, within the&nbsp;DKnife&nbsp;code, when reporting user activities back to the remote&nbsp;C2&nbsp;server, multiple messages are&nbsp;labelled&nbsp;in Simplified Chinese to&nbsp;indicate&nbsp;the types of activities.&nbsp;</p><figure class="kg-card kg-image-card kg-card-hascaption"><img src="https://blog.talosintelligence.com/content/images/2026/02/Screenshot-2026-02-03-at-15.31.19.png" class="kg-image" alt="" loading="lazy" width="928" height="364" srcset="https://blog.talosintelligence.com/content/images/size/w600/2026/02/Screenshot-2026-02-03-at-15.31.19.png 600w, https://blog.talosintelligence.com/content/images/2026/02/Screenshot-2026-02-03-at-15.31.19.png 928w" sizes="(min-width: 720px) 720px"><figcaption><span style="white-space: pre-wrap;">Figure 3.&nbsp;Simplified Chinese message embedded in the code and sent to remote&nbsp;C2.</span></figcaption></figure><h2 id="dknife-a-gateway-monitoring-and-aitm-framework">DKnife: A&nbsp;gateway&nbsp;monitoring and&nbsp;AitM&nbsp;framework&nbsp;</h2><p>DKnife&nbsp;is a full-featured gateway monitoring framework composed of&nbsp;seven&nbsp;ELF components that perform traffic manipulation across a target network. In addition to the seven ELF components that provide the core functionality, the framework comes with a list of configuration files (see Appendix for the full list), self-signed certificates, phishing templates, forged HTTP responses for hijacking and phishing, log files, and backdoor binaries.&nbsp;</p><p>&nbsp;The framework is designed to work with backdoors installed on compromised devices. Its key capabilities include serving update&nbsp;C2&nbsp;for the backdoors,&nbsp;DNS hijacking,&nbsp;hijacking Android application&nbsp;updates&nbsp;and&nbsp;binary&nbsp;downloads,&nbsp;delivering&nbsp;ShadowPad&nbsp;and&nbsp;DarkNimbus&nbsp;backdoors,&nbsp;selectively disrupting security-product&nbsp;traffic&nbsp;and exfiltrating user activity to remote C2 servers. The following sections highlight&nbsp;DKnife’s&nbsp;key capabilities and explain how its seven ELF binaries work together to implement them.&nbsp;</p><h3 id="targeted-platform">Targeted&nbsp;platform&nbsp;</h3><p>DKnife&nbsp;binaries are 64-bit Linux (x86-64) ELF implants that run on Linux-based devices. One of the components&nbsp;remote.bin&nbsp;imports the library&nbsp;"libcrypto.so.10",&nbsp;indicating&nbsp;it targets CentOS/RHEL-based platforms. Configuration elements such as&nbsp;PPPoE, VLAN tagging, a bridged interface (br0), and adjustable MTU and MAC parameters suggest that&nbsp;DKnife&nbsp;is tailored for edge or router devices running Linux-based firmware.&nbsp;&nbsp;</p><figure class="kg-card kg-image-card kg-card-hascaption"><img src="https://blog.talosintelligence.com/content/images/2026/02/Screenshot-2026-02-03-at-15.24.53-1-1.png" class="kg-image" alt="" loading="lazy" width="550" height="411"><figcaption><span style="white-space: pre-wrap;">Figure 4.&nbsp;wxha.conf&nbsp;config file.&nbsp;</span></figcaption></figure><h3 id="key-capabilities">Key capabilities&nbsp;</h3><p>The&nbsp;Deep Packet Inspection (DPI)&nbsp;logic and modular design of&nbsp;DKnife&nbsp;enable operators to conduct traffic monitoring campaigns ranging from covert monitoring of user activity to active in-line attacks that replace legitimate downloads with malicious payloads. The following sections&nbsp;highlight the framework’s key capabilities&nbsp;including:&nbsp;</p><ul><li>Serving C2 to Android and Windows&nbsp;DarkNimbus&nbsp;malware&nbsp;</li><li>DNS hijacking&nbsp;</li><li>Android Application binary update hijacking&nbsp;</li><li>Windows binary hijacking&nbsp;</li><li>Anti-virus traffic disruption&nbsp;</li><li>User activity monitoring&nbsp;</li></ul><h3 id="serving-updated-c2-to-the-android-and-windows-darknimbus-backdoors">Serving updated&nbsp;C2&nbsp;to&nbsp;the Android and Windows&nbsp;DarkNimbus&nbsp;backdoors&nbsp;</h3><p>In&nbsp;<a href="https://www.trendmicro.com/en_us/research/24/l/earth-minotaur.html" rel="noreferrer noopener"><u>previously published</u></a>&nbsp;research&nbsp;about the&nbsp;DarkNimbus&nbsp;backdoor, analysts noted that some samples communicated with their&nbsp;C2&nbsp;servers using a custom protocol,&nbsp;leading&nbsp;to the hypothesis that the backdoor operated within an&nbsp;AiTM&nbsp;environment.&nbsp;Talos'&nbsp;discovery of&nbsp;DKnife&nbsp;validates this assessment.&nbsp;</p><p>DKnife&nbsp;is designed to work with both Android and Windows variants of&nbsp;DarkNimbus.&nbsp;For the&nbsp;Windows version, the&nbsp;dknife.bin&nbsp;component&nbsp;inspects UDP traffic and sends them to port 8005. When it&nbsp;identifies&nbsp;a request&nbsp;containing&nbsp;the string marker&nbsp;<code>DKGETMMHOST</code>, it constructs and returns a response specifying the&nbsp;C2&nbsp;server address. The response includes two parameters:&nbsp;DKMMHOST&nbsp;and&nbsp;DKFESN. The&nbsp;DKMMHOST&nbsp;value is read from&nbsp;DKnife’s&nbsp;configuration file ("/dksoft/conf/server.conf"), which&nbsp;contains&nbsp;the line&nbsp;<code>MMHOST URL=[value]</code>. The&nbsp;DKFESN&nbsp;value&nbsp;represents&nbsp;a device identifier that&nbsp;DKnife&nbsp;retrieves from an internal server&nbsp;located&nbsp;at&nbsp;"192.168.92.92:8080".&nbsp;&nbsp;</p><figure class="kg-card kg-image-card kg-card-hascaption"><img src="https://blog.talosintelligence.com/content/images/2026/02/data-src-image-88dbfb04-7017-4f2b-b244-2a6682417418-1.png" class="kg-image" alt="" loading="lazy" width="600" height="461" srcset="https://blog.talosintelligence.com/content/images/2026/02/data-src-image-88dbfb04-7017-4f2b-b244-2a6682417418-1.png 600w"><figcaption><span style="white-space: pre-wrap;">Figure&nbsp;5.&nbsp;Code excerpt from&nbsp;DKnife&nbsp;showing the handler for “Obtain&nbsp;C2” requests from the Windows version of&nbsp;DarkNimbus.</span></figcaption></figure><p>For the Android variants, the backdoor attempts to contact a Baidu URL&nbsp;"http[:]//fanyi.baidu[.]com/query_config_dk"&nbsp;to retrieve its&nbsp;C2&nbsp;information. This URL does not return any response from Baidu itself; rather, it serves as a recognizable trigger for&nbsp;DKnife, which intercepts the request and injects the&nbsp;C2&nbsp;response.&nbsp;&nbsp;</p><figure class="kg-card kg-image-card kg-card-hascaption"><img src="https://blog.talosintelligence.com/content/images/2026/02/data-src-image-ace144fb-f065-43f3-a1f2-8636f7179a40-1.png" class="kg-image" alt="" loading="lazy" width="600" height="501" srcset="https://blog.talosintelligence.com/content/images/2026/02/data-src-image-ace144fb-f065-43f3-a1f2-8636f7179a40-1.png 600w"><figcaption><i><em class="italic" style="white-space: pre-wrap;">Figure</em></i> <i><em class="italic" style="white-space: pre-wrap;">6.</em></i> <i><em class="italic" style="white-space: pre-wrap;">Code from Android</em></i> <i><em class="italic" style="white-space: pre-wrap;">DarkNimbus</em></i> <i><em class="italic" style="white-space: pre-wrap;">sample</em></i> <i><em class="italic" style="white-space: pre-wrap;">e50247787d2e12c1e8743210a0c0e562cf694744436d93920a037d2f927f533.</em></i></figcaption></figure><figure class="kg-card kg-image-card kg-card-hascaption"><img src="https://blog.talosintelligence.com/content/images/2026/02/data-src-image-aee3850c-c8aa-4d7d-a78d-99c047158575.png" class="kg-image" alt="" loading="lazy" width="901" height="334" srcset="https://blog.talosintelligence.com/content/images/size/w600/2026/02/data-src-image-aee3850c-c8aa-4d7d-a78d-99c047158575.png 600w, https://blog.talosintelligence.com/content/images/2026/02/data-src-image-aee3850c-c8aa-4d7d-a78d-99c047158575.png 901w" sizes="(min-width: 720px) 720px"><figcaption><i><em class="italic" style="white-space: pre-wrap;">Figure</em></i> <i><em class="italic" style="white-space: pre-wrap;">7.</em></i> <i><em class="italic" style="white-space: pre-wrap;">Code in</em></i> <i><em class="italic" style="white-space: pre-wrap;">DKnife</em></i> <i><em class="italic" style="white-space: pre-wrap;">for handling “Obtain</em></i> <i><em class="italic" style="white-space: pre-wrap;">C2” request from Android version of</em></i> <i><em class="italic" style="white-space: pre-wrap;">DarkNimbus.</em></i></figcaption></figure><h3 id="dns-hijacking">DNS&nbsp;hijacking&nbsp;</h3><p>The&nbsp;DKnife&nbsp;framework relies on two main configuration files to control its DNS-based hijacking and attack logic. The&nbsp;<code>dns.conf</code>&nbsp;file defines the global keyword-to-IP mapping rules and framework parameters used for DNS interception. The<em>&nbsp;</em><code>perdns.conf</code><em>&nbsp;</em>file extends this by defining per-target or campaign-specific DNS attack tasks, including timing parameters such as interval and duration for each attack. In the archive we obtained from the&nbsp;C2&nbsp;server, only&nbsp;<code>perdns.conf</code>&nbsp;was present; it&nbsp;contained&nbsp;a template for setup rather than active attack data.&nbsp;</p><figure class="kg-card kg-image-card kg-card-hascaption"><img src="https://blog.talosintelligence.com/content/images/2026/02/data-src-image-f35f8722-3b35-4b7e-a7c4-e494f95058a6.png" class="kg-image" alt="" loading="lazy" width="901" height="90" srcset="https://blog.talosintelligence.com/content/images/size/w600/2026/02/data-src-image-f35f8722-3b35-4b7e-a7c4-e494f95058a6.png 600w, https://blog.talosintelligence.com/content/images/2026/02/data-src-image-f35f8722-3b35-4b7e-a7c4-e494f95058a6.png 901w" sizes="(min-width: 720px) 720px"><figcaption><i><em class="italic" style="white-space: pre-wrap;">Figure</em></i> <i><em class="italic" style="white-space: pre-wrap;">8.</em></i> <i><em class="italic" style="white-space: pre-wrap;">Perdns.conf</em></i> <i><em class="italic" style="white-space: pre-wrap;">template.</em></i></figcaption></figure><p>DKnife&nbsp;supports both&nbsp;IPv4 and IPv6&nbsp;DNS hijacking:&nbsp;</p><ul><li>IPv4 (A) DNS hijacking:<ul><li>For configured&nbsp;domains:&nbsp;replies with the per-domain IPv4 from&nbsp;<code>dns.conf</code>&nbsp;</li><li>For test.com: replies with&nbsp;<code>8.8.8.8</code>&nbsp;(and logs)&nbsp;</li><li>For JD-related domains (“api.m.jd.com”,&nbsp;“beta-api.m.jd.com”,&nbsp;“api.jd.co.th”,&nbsp;or&nbsp;“beta-api.jd.co.th”): replies with&nbsp;<code>10.3.3.3&nbsp;</code></li></ul></li><li>IPv6 (AAAA) DNS hijacking: <ul><li>For configured domains and for test.com: replies with fixed IPv6 IP&nbsp;<code>240e:a03:a03:303:a03:303:a03:303</code>&nbsp;(crafted)&nbsp;</li></ul></li></ul><p>The private IP address&nbsp;<code>10.3.3.3</code>&nbsp;belongs to the local interface created by the&nbsp;<code>yitiji.bin</code>&nbsp;component&nbsp;in&nbsp;DKnife.&nbsp;DKnife&nbsp;uses the&nbsp;local interface for delivering malicious binaries (see&nbsp;the following section). The crafted AAAA response is not an actual public address. When&nbsp;DKnife&nbsp;sees traffic addressed to that crafted IPv6, it checks the last 8 bytes of the address and converts it to the local interface address&nbsp;<code>10.3.3.3</code>.&nbsp;&nbsp;</p><p>The code also specially tempers the domains associated with mail services. It takes the queried domain,&nbsp;removes any trailing&nbsp;period&nbsp;if present, then splits on&nbsp;“.”&nbsp;and extracts the leftmost label&nbsp;(e.g.,&nbsp;“mail.example.com”&nbsp;into&nbsp;“mail”).&nbsp;It&nbsp;then looks up&nbsp;that label&nbsp;in the same per-domain configuration.&nbsp;Once&nbsp;the&nbsp;attack flag&nbsp;is&nbsp;enabled and the cooldown window has elapsed, it&nbsp;immediately&nbsp;injects a configured response to replace the&nbsp;original response.&nbsp;&nbsp;</p><h3 id="android-application-binary-update-hijacking">Android&nbsp;application&nbsp;binary&nbsp;update&nbsp;hijacking&nbsp;</h3><figure class="kg-card kg-image-card kg-card-hascaption"><img src="https://blog.talosintelligence.com/content/images/2026/02/dknife-android--1-.jpg" class="kg-image" alt="" loading="lazy" width="1833" height="1172" srcset="https://blog.talosintelligence.com/content/images/size/w600/2026/02/dknife-android--1-.jpg 600w, https://blog.talosintelligence.com/content/images/size/w1000/2026/02/dknife-android--1-.jpg 1000w, https://blog.talosintelligence.com/content/images/size/w1600/2026/02/dknife-android--1-.jpg 1600w, https://blog.talosintelligence.com/content/images/2026/02/dknife-android--1-.jpg 1833w" sizes="(min-width: 720px) 720px"><figcaption><i><em class="italic" style="white-space: pre-wrap;">Figure</em></i> <i><em class="italic" style="white-space: pre-wrap;">9.</em></i> <i><em class="italic" style="white-space: pre-wrap;">Android APK download hijacking workflow.</em></i></figcaption></figure><p>DKnife&nbsp;can hijack and replace Android application updates by intercepting the update manifest requests. When an Android application sends an APK update manifest request,&nbsp;DKnife&nbsp;intercepts it, consults the configuration file, and selects the corresponding JSON response file to reply. This response&nbsp;contains&nbsp;a download URL redirecting to the URL of address&nbsp;<code>10.3.3.3</code>, which&nbsp;DKnife&nbsp;recognizes and routes to the&nbsp;<code>yitiji.bin</code>&nbsp;created Local&nbsp;Area Network (LAN) to deliver malware instead of the legitimate update APK.&nbsp;</p><p>&nbsp;The configuration file&nbsp;<code>/dksoft/conf/url.cfg</code>&nbsp;defines the rules and responses used for traffic blocking, phishing on Android and Windows platforms,&nbsp;executable file (<em>.</em>exe)&nbsp;&nbsp;hijacking, and credential-phishing page responses. The file follows the format:&nbsp;<code>[Request URL] [Response JSON file]</code>&nbsp;as shown in&nbsp;Figure 11.&nbsp;</p><figure class="kg-card kg-image-card kg-card-hascaption"><img src="https://blog.talosintelligence.com/content/images/2026/02/Screenshot-2026-02-03-at-15.44.35.png" class="kg-image" alt="" loading="lazy" width="1162" height="308" srcset="https://blog.talosintelligence.com/content/images/size/w600/2026/02/Screenshot-2026-02-03-at-15.44.35.png 600w, https://blog.talosintelligence.com/content/images/size/w1000/2026/02/Screenshot-2026-02-03-at-15.44.35.png 1000w, https://blog.talosintelligence.com/content/images/2026/02/Screenshot-2026-02-03-at-15.44.35.png 1162w" sizes="(min-width: 720px) 720px"><figcaption><span style="white-space: pre-wrap;">Figure 10.&nbsp;Configuration file&nbsp;</span><code spellcheck="false" style="white-space: pre-wrap;"><span>url.cfg</span></code><span style="white-space: pre-wrap;">&nbsp;defines the&nbsp;targeted sites and&nbsp;update manifest file response&nbsp;DKnife&nbsp;is sending to the requested URL.</span></figcaption></figure><p>Within the&nbsp;<code>/bin/html/dkay-scripts</code>&nbsp;folder of the&nbsp;DKnife&nbsp;archive, there are 185 JSON files configured to hijack applications.&nbsp;The targeted applications are mostly popular&nbsp;Chinese-language&nbsp;services&nbsp;(some only available in China), including news media, video streaming, image editing apps, e-commerce platforms, taxi-service platforms, gaming,&nbsp;and&nbsp;pornography&nbsp;video streaming, among others. An example response used to hijack&nbsp;a Chinese photo editing&nbsp;application update request is shown below:&nbsp;</p><figure class="kg-card kg-image-card kg-card-hascaption"><img src="https://blog.talosintelligence.com/content/images/2026/02/carbon-1.png" class="kg-image" alt="" loading="lazy" width="700" height="851" srcset="https://blog.talosintelligence.com/content/images/size/w600/2026/02/carbon-1.png 600w, https://blog.talosintelligence.com/content/images/2026/02/carbon-1.png 700w"><figcaption><span style="white-space: pre-wrap;">Figure 11.&nbsp;The response manifest file (</span><code spellcheck="false" style="white-space: pre-wrap;"><span>11184.json</span></code><span style="white-space: pre-wrap;">) for hijacking the APK download</span></figcaption></figure><h2 id="windows-binary-hijacking-for-delivering-shadowpad-and-darknimbus">Windows&nbsp;binary&nbsp;hijacking for&nbsp;delivering&nbsp;Shadowpad&nbsp;and&nbsp;DarkNimbus&nbsp;</h2><p>In addition to Android update hijacking,&nbsp;DKnife&nbsp;also supports hijacking of Windows and other binary downloads. The hijacking rules are set up during initialization.&nbsp;DKnife&nbsp;attempts to read the rules configuration file at&nbsp;<code>/dksoft/conf/rules.aes</code>&nbsp;and decrypts it using a variant of the Tiny Encryption Algorithm (TEA) algorithm employed by Tencent’s older OICQ/QQ login protocols, commonly referred to as&nbsp;<a href="https://github.com/yuleniwo/qqtea" rel="noreferrer noopener"><u>QQ TEA</u></a>.&nbsp;DKnife&nbsp;decrypts the file with a key&nbsp;<code>dianke0123456789</code>,&nbsp;and&nbsp;saves&nbsp;the decrypted file as&nbsp;<code>rules.conf</code>.&nbsp;&nbsp;</p><figure class="kg-card kg-image-card kg-card-hascaption"><img src="https://blog.talosintelligence.com/content/images/2026/02/data-src-image-f19e5fee-d174-4b93-b858-0c9594359810.png" class="kg-image" alt="" loading="lazy" width="798" height="621" srcset="https://blog.talosintelligence.com/content/images/size/w600/2026/02/data-src-image-f19e5fee-d174-4b93-b858-0c9594359810.png 600w, https://blog.talosintelligence.com/content/images/2026/02/data-src-image-f19e5fee-d174-4b93-b858-0c9594359810.png 798w" sizes="(min-width: 720px) 720px"><figcaption><i><em class="italic" style="white-space: pre-wrap;">Figure 12.</em></i> <i><em class="italic" style="white-space: pre-wrap;">QQ TEA decipher algorithm</em></i></figcaption></figure><p>Talos&nbsp;did not obtain the&nbsp;<code>rules.aes</code>&nbsp;file from the archive we downloaded.&nbsp;However, based on the code analysis,&nbsp;<code>rules.conf</code>&nbsp;is the configuration to define what requests to match, what to send back, when to throttle and tracking the response. The&nbsp;rules include the following information:&nbsp;&nbsp;</p>

<!--kg-card-end: html-->
<p>After reading the rules into a data structure in the memory, the&nbsp;<code>rules.conf</code>&nbsp;file is&nbsp;deleted&nbsp;on the device. When an HTTP request’s Host and URI match the configured rule,&nbsp;DKnife&nbsp;evaluates the rule’s duration and interval timers to&nbsp;determine&nbsp;whether to trigger. If the rule fires and the requested filename&nbsp;has&nbsp;a matching extension (e.g.,&nbsp;“.exe”,&nbsp;“.rar”,&nbsp;“.zip”,&nbsp;or&nbsp;“.apk”),&nbsp;DKnife&nbsp;forges an HTTP 302 redirect whose Location URL is taken from the rule’s data field.&nbsp;</p><figure class="kg-card kg-image-card kg-card-hascaption"><img src="https://blog.talosintelligence.com/content/images/2026/02/data-src-image-10a2f519-3931-47d1-9e53-dcb8210b15f0.png" class="kg-image" alt="" loading="lazy" width="901" height="216" srcset="https://blog.talosintelligence.com/content/images/size/w600/2026/02/data-src-image-10a2f519-3931-47d1-9e53-dcb8210b15f0.png 600w, https://blog.talosintelligence.com/content/images/2026/02/data-src-image-10a2f519-3931-47d1-9e53-dcb8210b15f0.png 901w" sizes="(min-width: 720px) 720px"><figcaption><i><em class="italic" style="white-space: pre-wrap;">Figure 13.</em></i> <i><em class="italic" style="white-space: pre-wrap;">Code to</em></i> <i><em class="italic" style="white-space: pre-wrap;">match on the binary download and respond with HTTP 302.</em></i></figcaption></figure><p>If the&nbsp;binary&nbsp;download URL matches a specific pattern (“.exe”&nbsp;extension after the query symbol), the file name is replaced with&nbsp;<code>install.exe</code>.&nbsp;</p><figure class="kg-card kg-image-card kg-card-hascaption"><img src="https://blog.talosintelligence.com/content/images/2026/02/data-src-image-791d2c52-1daa-42a6-ac80-eedb9d5b347d.png" class="kg-image" alt="" loading="lazy" width="901" height="222" srcset="https://blog.talosintelligence.com/content/images/size/w600/2026/02/data-src-image-791d2c52-1daa-42a6-ac80-eedb9d5b347d.png 600w, https://blog.talosintelligence.com/content/images/2026/02/data-src-image-791d2c52-1daa-42a6-ac80-eedb9d5b347d.png 901w" sizes="(min-width: 720px) 720px"><figcaption><i><em class="italic" style="white-space: pre-wrap;">Figure 14.</em></i> <i><em class="italic" style="white-space: pre-wrap;">Code to replace</em></i> <i><em class="italic" style="white-space: pre-wrap;">.exe</em></i> <i><em class="italic" style="white-space: pre-wrap;">download file name.</em></i></figcaption></figure><h3 id="shadowpad-and-darknimbus-backdoors">Shadowpad&nbsp;and&nbsp;DarkNimbus&nbsp;backdoors&nbsp;</h3><p>The&nbsp;<code>install.exe</code>&nbsp;file (SHA256: <code>2550aa4c4bc0a020ec4b16973df271b81118a7abea77f77fec2f575a32dc3444</code>) is found in the downloaded archive under path&nbsp;<code>/dkay-scripts/</code>. It is a&nbsp;RAR&nbsp;self extraction&nbsp;package&nbsp;containing&nbsp;three binaries, that are actually&nbsp;ShadowPad&nbsp;and the&nbsp;DarkNimbus&nbsp;backdoor, which both being&nbsp;reported&nbsp;[<a href="https://blog.talosintelligence.com/chinese-hacking-group-apt41-compromised-taiwanese-government-affiliated-research-institute-with-shadowpad-and-cobaltstrike-2/" rel="noreferrer noopener"><u>1</u></a>,<a href="https://www.trendmicro.com/en_us/research/24/l/earth-minotaur.html" rel="noreferrer noopener"><u>2</u></a>]&nbsp;used by&nbsp;China-nexus&nbsp;threat&nbsp;actors. When launched, the legitimate&nbsp;.exe&nbsp;(<code>TosBtKbd.exe</code>) sideloads the&nbsp;ShadowPad&nbsp;DLL loader (<code>TosBtKbd.dll</code>), which then loads the&nbsp;DarkNimbus&nbsp;DLL backdoor&nbsp;(<code>TosBtKbdLayer.dll</code>). That&nbsp;DarkNimbus&nbsp;backdoor calls out to the Cloudflare DNS address&nbsp;<code>1.1.1.1</code>, which&nbsp;DKnife&nbsp;intercepts to return the real&nbsp;C2&nbsp;IP.&nbsp;</p><figure class="kg-card kg-image-card kg-card-hascaption"><img src="https://blog.talosintelligence.com/content/images/2026/02/dknife-backdoor--1--2.jpg" class="kg-image" alt="" loading="lazy" width="1540" height="1554" srcset="https://blog.talosintelligence.com/content/images/size/w600/2026/02/dknife-backdoor--1--2.jpg 600w, https://blog.talosintelligence.com/content/images/size/w1000/2026/02/dknife-backdoor--1--2.jpg 1000w, https://blog.talosintelligence.com/content/images/2026/02/dknife-backdoor--1--2.jpg 1540w" sizes="(min-width: 720px) 720px"><figcaption><span style="white-space: pre-wrap;">Figure 15.&nbsp;Shadowpad&nbsp;and&nbsp;DarkNimbus&nbsp;backdoor delivered by&nbsp;DKnife.</span></figcaption></figure><p>The&nbsp;Shadowpad&nbsp;sample has not been previously reported but is&nbsp;very similar&nbsp;to a&nbsp;<a href="https://www.trendmicro.com/en_us/research/24/l/earth-minotaur.html" rel="noreferrer noopener"><u>previously reported sample</u></a>. Although it uses a different unpacking XOR seed key, it employs the same unpacking algorithm.&nbsp;</p><figure class="kg-card kg-image-card kg-card-hascaption"><img src="https://blog.talosintelligence.com/content/images/2026/02/data-src-image-13406b87-4003-48b4-9e63-021c19890c9d-1.png" class="kg-image" alt="" loading="lazy" width="361" height="200"><figcaption><span style="white-space: pre-wrap;">Figure 16.&nbsp;Unpacking algorithm used in the&nbsp;Shadowpad&nbsp;loader sample (SHA256:&nbsp;</span><code spellcheck="false" style="white-space: pre-wrap;"><span>43891d3898a54a132d198be47a44a8d4856201fa7a87f3f850432ba9e038893a</span></code><span style="white-space: pre-wrap;">)</span></figcaption></figure><figure class="kg-card kg-image-card kg-card-hascaption"><img src="https://blog.talosintelligence.com/content/images/2026/02/data-src-image-d7fe3d7d-7c77-44a1-8a3d-7713d3455594-1.png" class="kg-image" alt="" loading="lazy" width="409" height="312"><figcaption><span style="white-space: pre-wrap;">Figure 17.&nbsp;Unpacking algorithm used in the&nbsp;Trend Micro’s sample (SHA256:&nbsp;</span><code spellcheck="false" style="white-space: pre-wrap;"><span>c59509018bbbe5482452a205513a2eb5d86004369309818ece7eba7a462ef854</span></code><span style="white-space: pre-wrap;">)</span></figcaption></figure><p>The&nbsp;Shadowpad&nbsp;samples (both&nbsp;<em>.exe</em>&nbsp;and&nbsp;<em>.dll</em>)&nbsp;are signed with two certificates both issued from the signer “四川奇雨网络科技有限公司”.&nbsp;This is a company&nbsp;located&nbsp;in Sichuan Chengdu, China&nbsp;specialised in developing computer software and providing network communication devices, according to&nbsp;<a href="https://m.zhipin.com/gongsis/f99f4a7246410b5f0nF63NW9E1I~.html" rel="noreferrer noopener"><u>publicly</u>&nbsp;<u>available information</u></a>.&nbsp;Pivoting on this signer,&nbsp;Talos&nbsp;found 17 samples&nbsp;that&nbsp;contain&nbsp;the&nbsp;Shadowpad&nbsp;and&nbsp;DarkNimbus&nbsp;backdoor.&nbsp;&nbsp;</p><h2 id="anti-virus-traffic-disruption">Anti-virus&nbsp;traffic&nbsp;disruption&nbsp;</h2><p>The&nbsp;DKnife&nbsp;traffic inspection module actively&nbsp;identifies&nbsp;and interferes with communications from&nbsp;antivirus and PC-management products. It detects 360 Total Security by searching HTTP headers (e.g., the&nbsp;DPUname&nbsp;header in GET requests or the x-360-ver header in POST requests) and by matching known service domain names.&nbsp;When a match is found,&nbsp;the module drops or otherwise disrupts the traffic with&nbsp;the&nbsp;crafted TCP RST packet. It similarly looks for and disrupts connections to Tencent services and PC-management endpoints.&nbsp;</p><p>Recognized Tencent-related domains:&nbsp;</p><ul><li><code>dlied6.qq.com&nbsp;</code></li><li><code>pcmgr.qq.com&nbsp;</code></li><li><code>pc.qq.com&nbsp;</code></li><li><code>www.qq.com/q.cgi&nbsp;</code></li></ul><p>Keywords used&nbsp;to match 360 Total Security-related domains:&nbsp;</p><ul><li><code>360.cn&nbsp;</code></li><li><code>360safe&nbsp;</code></li><li><code>qihucdn&nbsp;</code></li><li><code>duba.net&nbsp;</code></li><li><code>mbdlog.iqiyi.com&nbsp;</code></li></ul><h2 id="user-activity-monitoring">User&nbsp;activity&nbsp;monitoring&nbsp;</h2><p>DKnife&nbsp;inspects traffic to&nbsp;monitor&nbsp;and report user’s network activity to its remote&nbsp;C2&nbsp;in real&nbsp;time. Observed telemetry categories include messaging (Signal and WeChat activities including voice/video calls, sent texts, received images, in-app article views), shopping, news consumption, map searches, video streaming, gaming, dating,&nbsp;taxi&nbsp;and rideshare&nbsp;requests, mail checking, and other user actions. Most of the activity reports are triggered by&nbsp;monitoring&nbsp;the request to service/platform domains or URLs. When reporting, the code sends a corresponding embedded message&nbsp;representing&nbsp;the reported activity. For&nbsp;example,&nbsp;Figure 18&nbsp;shows the code to report Signal messaging activities. The message sent to remote&nbsp;C2&nbsp;translates to “Using Signal encryption chat APP”.&nbsp;</p><figure class="kg-card kg-image-card kg-card-hascaption"><img src="https://blog.talosintelligence.com/content/images/2026/02/data-src-image-b8cdb6e6-417b-48df-a8b4-c1f4539d0f71.png" class="kg-image" alt="" loading="lazy" width="619" height="325" srcset="https://blog.talosintelligence.com/content/images/size/w600/2026/02/data-src-image-b8cdb6e6-417b-48df-a8b4-c1f4539d0f71.png 600w, https://blog.talosintelligence.com/content/images/2026/02/data-src-image-b8cdb6e6-417b-48df-a8b4-c1f4539d0f71.png 619w"><figcaption><i><em class="italic" style="white-space: pre-wrap;">Figure 18.</em></i> <i><em class="italic" style="white-space: pre-wrap;">Code for reporting Signal communication</em></i></figcaption></figure><p>The&nbsp;table&nbsp;below&nbsp;shows some of the observed telemetry categories and the embedded messages.&nbsp;&nbsp;</p>

<!--kg-card-end: html-->
<h3 id="emailplatforms-credential-harvesting-and-phishing">Email/platforms&nbsp;credential&nbsp;harvesting&nbsp;and phishing&nbsp;</h3><p>DKnife&nbsp;can harvest credentials from&nbsp;a major Chinese email provider&nbsp;and host phishing pages for other services.&nbsp;&nbsp;For&nbsp;harvesting email credentials, the&nbsp;<code>sslmm.bin</code>&nbsp;component&nbsp;presents its own TLS certificate to clients,&nbsp;terminates&nbsp;and decrypts POP3/IMAP connections, and inspects the plaintext stream to extract usernames and passwords. Extracted credentials are tagged with&nbsp;"PASSWORD",&nbsp;forwarded&nbsp;to the&nbsp;<code>postapi.bin</code>&nbsp;component, and&nbsp;ultimately relayed&nbsp;to remote&nbsp;C2&nbsp;servers.&nbsp;</p><figure class="kg-card kg-image-card kg-card-hascaption"><img src="https://blog.talosintelligence.com/content/images/2026/02/data-src-image-ed15ddbc-e5a7-4fd7-8257-8b1234795900.png" class="kg-image" alt="" loading="lazy" width="705" height="219" srcset="https://blog.talosintelligence.com/content/images/size/w600/2026/02/data-src-image-ed15ddbc-e5a7-4fd7-8257-8b1234795900.png 600w, https://blog.talosintelligence.com/content/images/2026/02/data-src-image-ed15ddbc-e5a7-4fd7-8257-8b1234795900.png 705w"><figcaption><i><em class="italic" style="white-space: pre-wrap;">Figure</em></i> <i><em class="italic" style="white-space: pre-wrap;">19.</em></i> <i><em class="italic" style="white-space: pre-wrap;">Code to</em></i> <i><em class="italic" style="white-space: pre-wrap;">forward</em></i> <i><em class="italic" style="white-space: pre-wrap;">password.</em></i></figcaption></figure><p>DKnife&nbsp;can also serve phishing pages. The phishing routes are defined in&nbsp;<code>url.cfg</code>, and several phishing templates were discovered under&nbsp;<code>/dkay-scripts/</code>. All discovered pages&nbsp;submit&nbsp;harvested passwords to endpoints whose paths end with&nbsp;<code>dklogin.html</code>; however, no&nbsp;<code>dklogin.html</code>&nbsp;file was found in the local script directory.&nbsp;</p><figure class="kg-card kg-image-card kg-card-hascaption"><img src="https://blog.talosintelligence.com/content/images/2026/02/data-src-image-27222d83-302e-423b-9e7c-7b4aefa5d41c.png" class="kg-image" alt="" loading="lazy" width="901" height="271" srcset="https://blog.talosintelligence.com/content/images/size/w600/2026/02/data-src-image-27222d83-302e-423b-9e7c-7b4aefa5d41c.png 600w, https://blog.talosintelligence.com/content/images/2026/02/data-src-image-27222d83-302e-423b-9e7c-7b4aefa5d41c.png 901w" sizes="(min-width: 720px) 720px"><figcaption><i><em class="italic" style="white-space: pre-wrap;">Figure</em></i><span style="white-space: pre-wrap;">&nbsp;20</span><i><em class="italic" style="white-space: pre-wrap;">.</em></i> <i><em class="italic" style="white-space: pre-wrap;">Phishing page setup.</em></i></figcaption></figure><p>In addition to the capabilities described above, Talos observed&nbsp;DKnife&nbsp;functions that may target IoT devices. Talos is coordinating with the device&nbsp;vendor&nbsp;on mitigations.&nbsp;</p><h2 id="the-dknife-downloader">The&nbsp;DKnife&nbsp;downloader&nbsp;</h2><p>The ELF&nbsp;binary (<code>17a2dd45f9f57161b4cc40924296c4deab65beea447efb46d3178a9e76815d06</code>) we discovered from hunting is a downloader that downloads&nbsp;and performs&nbsp;initial setup for the&nbsp;DKnife&nbsp;framework. Upon execution, it&nbsp;attempts&nbsp;to load a configuration file from&nbsp;<code>/dksoft/conf/server.conf</code>&nbsp;to set up the&nbsp;C2&nbsp;server. The&nbsp;server.conf&nbsp;file&nbsp;contains&nbsp;the&nbsp;C2&nbsp;configuration in the format&nbsp;<code>UPDATE URL=[config]</code>. If the file does not exist, the binary defaults to the embedded C2 URL&nbsp;<code>http://47.93.54[.]134:8005/</code>.&nbsp;</p><p>After configuring the&nbsp;C2, the binary retrieves or generates a UUID for the host device based on the MAC addresses of its network interfaces and stores it in&nbsp;<code>/etc/diankeuuid</code>. The UUID follows the format&nbsp;<code>YYYYMMDDhhmmss[MAC1][MAC2]</code><em>&nbsp;</em>(e.g.,&nbsp;<code>20240219165234000c295de649</code>).&nbsp;The updater also stores a 32-character hexadecimal MD5 checksum in&nbsp;<code>/dksoft/conf/&lt;UUID&gt;.ini</code>, which is later used to verify updates from the&nbsp;C2&nbsp;server.&nbsp;&nbsp;</p><p>The code&nbsp;establishes&nbsp;persistence by&nbsp;modifying&nbsp;the&nbsp;<code>/etc/rc.local</code>&nbsp;file, a script commonly used to execute commands and scripts after the system boots and initializes services. The updater adds its commands between markers&nbsp;<code>#startdianke</code>&nbsp;and&nbsp;<code>#enddianke</code>. It also copies the currently running executable into the&nbsp;<code>/dksoft/update/</code>&nbsp;directory and appends a corresponding entry to&nbsp;<code>/dksoft/update/[executable path]</code> auto&nbsp;to ensure the binary&nbsp;runs automatically&nbsp;each time the system starts.&nbsp;</p><p>After creating the folders for&nbsp;DKnife&nbsp;deployment, the downloader fetches the&nbsp;DKnife&nbsp;archive from the&nbsp;C2&nbsp;and launches every binary in&nbsp;<code>/dksoft/bin/</code> using&nbsp;<code>nohup&nbsp;[filepath] 2&gt;/dev/null 1&gt;/dev/null &amp;</code>. The folder&nbsp;contains&nbsp;seven binaries, each performing a distinct role within the&nbsp;DKnife&nbsp;framework.&nbsp;</p><h2 id="dknife%E2%80%99s-seven-components">DKnife’s&nbsp;seven&nbsp;components&nbsp;</h2><p>The seven implants in&nbsp;DKnife&nbsp;serve the purpose of&nbsp;DPI engine, data reporting, reverse proxy for&nbsp;AitM&nbsp;attack,&nbsp;malicious APK download,&nbsp;framework update, traffic forwarding,&nbsp;and building P2P communication channel with the remote&nbsp;C2.&nbsp;A summary of the components and their roles are listed in the table below:</p>

<!--kg-card-end: html-->
<p>The graph below&nbsp;shows how the seven&nbsp;DKnife&nbsp;components work&nbsp;together.&nbsp;</p><figure class="kg-card kg-image-card kg-card-hascaption"><img src="https://blog.talosintelligence.com/content/images/2026/02/dknife-components--1-.jpg" class="kg-image" alt="" loading="lazy" width="2000" height="1407" srcset="https://blog.talosintelligence.com/content/images/size/w600/2026/02/dknife-components--1-.jpg 600w, https://blog.talosintelligence.com/content/images/size/w1000/2026/02/dknife-components--1-.jpg 1000w, https://blog.talosintelligence.com/content/images/size/w1600/2026/02/dknife-components--1-.jpg 1600w, https://blog.talosintelligence.com/content/images/2026/02/dknife-components--1-.jpg 2299w" sizes="(min-width: 720px) 720px"><figcaption><i><em class="italic" style="white-space: pre-wrap;">Figure 21.</em></i> <i><em class="italic" style="white-space: pre-wrap;">Functions of seven</em></i> <i><em class="italic" style="white-space: pre-wrap;">DKnife</em></i> <i><em class="italic" style="white-space: pre-wrap;">components.</em></i></figcaption></figure><h3 id="dknifebin">DKnife.bin&nbsp;</h3><p>The&nbsp;<code>dknife.bin</code>&nbsp;implant is the main&nbsp;component&nbsp;that acts as the brain of&nbsp;DKnife. It&nbsp;is in charge of&nbsp;all the packet inspection and attack logics, as&nbsp;described in the Key Capabilities&nbsp;section. Upon execution, the implant does some&nbsp;initial&nbsp;setup for the framework. It reads the configuration file&nbsp;<code>/dksoft/conf/wxha.conf</code>&nbsp;to search for the sniffing interface (<code>INPUT_ETH</code>)&nbsp;and attacker interface (<code>ATT_ETH</code>). If the config file is not presented, the default interface for both&nbsp;are&nbsp;<code>eth0</code>. It also reads configuration files for attacking rules and remote&nbsp;C2.&nbsp;&nbsp;</p><p>Throughout the&nbsp;packet inspection process,&nbsp;<code>dknife.bin</code>&nbsp;reports information including collected data, user’s activities, attack&nbsp;status&nbsp;and average throughput to the relay&nbsp;component&nbsp;<code>postapi.bin</code>&nbsp;listening at the 7788&nbsp;port&nbsp;on the device. The reporting packets are a 256-byte UDP datagram with a fixed seven bytes prefix&nbsp;<code>DK7788</code>. At offset 0x40 a label is attached, which&nbsp;represents&nbsp;types of the information (example types including DKIMSI for IMSI information, USERID for harvested user accounts,&nbsp;WECHAT&nbsp;for&nbsp;WeChat activities reporting, ATKRESULT&nbsp;for&nbsp;attack results,&nbsp;etc).&nbsp;Each type of reporting has the corresponding report value format. We listed some examples in the graph below.&nbsp;&nbsp;</p><figure class="kg-card kg-image-card kg-card-hascaption"><img src="https://blog.talosintelligence.com/content/images/2026/02/carbon--3-.png" class="kg-image" alt="" loading="lazy" width="1750" height="838" srcset="https://blog.talosintelligence.com/content/images/size/w600/2026/02/carbon--3-.png 600w, https://blog.talosintelligence.com/content/images/size/w1000/2026/02/carbon--3-.png 1000w, https://blog.talosintelligence.com/content/images/size/w1600/2026/02/carbon--3-.png 1600w, https://blog.talosintelligence.com/content/images/2026/02/carbon--3-.png 1750w" sizes="(min-width: 720px) 720px"><figcaption><span style="white-space: pre-wrap;">Figure&nbsp;22.&nbsp;Report UDP datagram send from&nbsp;</span><code spellcheck="false" style="white-space: pre-wrap;"><span>dknife.bin</span></code><span style="white-space: pre-wrap;">&nbsp;to&nbsp;</span><code spellcheck="false" style="white-space: pre-wrap;"><span>postapi.bin</span></code><span style="white-space: pre-wrap;">.</span></figcaption></figure><figure class="kg-card kg-image-card kg-card-hascaption"><img src="https://blog.talosintelligence.com/content/images/2026/02/dknife-reporting--2-.jpg" class="kg-image" alt="" loading="lazy" width="1609" height="1079" srcset="https://blog.talosintelligence.com/content/images/size/w600/2026/02/dknife-reporting--2-.jpg 600w, https://blog.talosintelligence.com/content/images/size/w1000/2026/02/dknife-reporting--2-.jpg 1000w, https://blog.talosintelligence.com/content/images/size/w1600/2026/02/dknife-reporting--2-.jpg 1600w, https://blog.talosintelligence.com/content/images/2026/02/dknife-reporting--2-.jpg 1609w" sizes="(min-width: 720px) 720px"><figcaption><i><em class="italic" style="white-space: pre-wrap;">Figure 23.</em></i> <i><em class="italic" style="white-space: pre-wrap;">Message reporting</em></i> <i><em class="italic" style="white-space: pre-wrap;">format.</em></i></figcaption></figure><h3 id="postapibin">Postapi.bin&nbsp;</h3><p>This is the data relay&nbsp;component&nbsp;in&nbsp;DKnife.&nbsp;It receives forwarded UDP&nbsp;dataframe&nbsp;from&nbsp;<code>dknife.bin</code>, processes,&nbsp;identifies,&nbsp;and labels the data and sends them to remote&nbsp;C2&nbsp;servers. When receiving the UDP&nbsp;dataframe, it&nbsp;validates&nbsp;the&nbsp;DK7788&nbsp;prefix and extracts device ID, MAC address, source and destination IPs and ports. It then exfiltrates more interesting data based on the rules defined in file&nbsp;<code>ssluserid.conf</code>. The file is a rulebook for defining the targeted services/platforms and the corresponding scrapping data. The rules define the following methods&nbsp;for scraping:&nbsp;</p><ul><li><code>get_url</code>: scrape a value from the URL of a GET request&nbsp;&nbsp;</li><li><code>get_cookie</code>: scrape from Cookie header of a GET&nbsp;&nbsp;</li><li><code>post_url</code>: scrape from the URL of a POST&nbsp;&nbsp;</li><li><code>post_cookie</code>: scrape from Cookie header of a POST&nbsp;&nbsp;</li><li><code>post_content</code>: scrape from the body of a POST&nbsp;&nbsp;</li></ul><p>Each rule also defines which data fields to collect. These include device IDs, phone numbers, IMEIs/IMSIs, MACs, UUIDs, IPs, usernames, etc.&nbsp;DKnife&nbsp;targets dozens of popular&nbsp;Chinese-language&nbsp;mobile and web apps, some of which are only available to Chinese users.&nbsp;Figure below&nbsp;shows part of the rules in the configuration file</p><figure class="kg-card kg-image-card kg-card-hascaption"><img src="https://blog.talosintelligence.com/content/images/2026/02/data-src-image-c6ea1853-1ec2-4f40-a8bf-cbaecec2faa9-1.png" class="kg-image" alt="" loading="lazy" width="901" height="68" srcset="https://blog.talosintelligence.com/content/images/size/w600/2026/02/data-src-image-c6ea1853-1ec2-4f40-a8bf-cbaecec2faa9-1.png 600w, https://blog.talosintelligence.com/content/images/2026/02/data-src-image-c6ea1853-1ec2-4f40-a8bf-cbaecec2faa9-1.png 901w" sizes="(min-width: 720px) 720px"><figcaption><i><em class="italic" style="white-space: pre-wrap;">Figure 24.</em></i> <i><em class="italic" style="white-space: pre-wrap;">Rules in</em></i> <i><code spellcheck="false" style="white-space: pre-wrap;"><em class="italic">ssluserid.conf</em></code></i><i><em class="italic" style="white-space: pre-wrap;">.</em></i></figcaption></figure><p><code>Postapi.bin</code>&nbsp;loads the configuration file&nbsp;<code>server.conf</code>&nbsp;to obtain the address of the remote&nbsp;C2&nbsp;server used for data exfiltration. If the file is missing, it defaults to&nbsp;<code>https://47.93.54[.]134:8003</code>. The&nbsp;component&nbsp;uses&nbsp;libcurl&nbsp;to send&nbsp;different types&nbsp;of exfiltrated and reporting data via HTTP POST requests to specific API endpoints. The following table lists the reporting URLs and the corresponding data transmitted.</p>

<!--kg-card-end: html-->
<p>The posted data always include a&nbsp;<code>dkimsi=&lt;IMSI&gt;</code>&nbsp;at the end of the data, which is the IMSI or mobile identifier extracted from the packets if available. The binary set a default IMSI&nbsp;<code>460110672021628</code>&nbsp;in the code, which is an IMSI with&nbsp;a&nbsp;China Telecom carrier.&nbsp;</p><h3 id="sslmmbin">Sslmm.bin&nbsp;</h3><p>This component acts as the reverse proxy server for the AitM attack and is implemented as a pre-configured, customized build of HAProxy. It loads its primary configuration from&nbsp;<code>sslmm.cfg</code>&nbsp;and performs request hijacking and replacement according to rules defined in&nbsp;<code>url.cfg</code>. Copies of hijacked traffic and execution results are encapsulated as UDP dataframes and sent to the&nbsp;<code>postapi.bin</code>&nbsp;component, similar to the behavior implemented in&nbsp;<code>dknife.bin</code>.&nbsp;</p><p>In addition to standard&nbsp;HAProxy&nbsp;proxying,&nbsp;<code>sslmm.bin</code>&nbsp;includes custom logic to inspect, log, exfiltrate, and conditionally rewrite client HTTP(S) requests after TLS termination. Content injection is primarily performed through HTTP request-line replacement, redirecting victims to attacker-controlled resources that are typically hosted under the&nbsp;<code>/dkay-scripts/</code>&nbsp;directory. The resulting telemetry and artifacts are then relayed via postapi.bin to remote C2 infrastructure.&nbsp;</p><p>Operationally, the HAProxy configuration terminates TLS on HTTPS and mail-over-TLS ports (443, 993, 995) using a self-signed certificate stored at <code>/dksoft/conf/server.pem</code>, and proxies the decrypted traffic to the appropriate backends. A management/statistics interface is exposed on&nbsp;<code>0.0.0.0:10800</code>&nbsp;and protected only by static credentials. Requests matching the <code>/dkay-scripts/</code> path are selectively downgraded to plain HTTP and routed to a local service at<em>&nbsp;<code>127.0.0.1:81</code>,</em>&nbsp;enabling response modification or injection before content is returned to the client.&nbsp;</p><p>This interception model depends on a key trust assumption: for the TLS MITM to be transparent, endpoints must accept the certificate chain presented by the gateway. One&nbsp;hypothesis is that the associated endpoint malware (given the broader DarkNimbus toolchain across Windows and Android) may be used to establish that trust or weaken certificate validation, enabling host-specific certificates to be presented during interception. However, we did not have the artifacts to confirm that such trust establishment or validation bypass is performed on victim devices.&nbsp;&nbsp;</p><figure class="kg-card kg-image-card kg-card-hascaption"><img src="https://blog.talosintelligence.com/content/images/2026/02/data-src-image-757b5c9b-0007-45ce-8eb5-6b0a2d074d9e.png" class="kg-image" alt="" loading="lazy" width="817" height="561" srcset="https://blog.talosintelligence.com/content/images/size/w600/2026/02/data-src-image-757b5c9b-0007-45ce-8eb5-6b0a2d074d9e.png 600w, https://blog.talosintelligence.com/content/images/2026/02/data-src-image-757b5c9b-0007-45ce-8eb5-6b0a2d074d9e.png 817w" sizes="(min-width: 720px) 720px"><figcaption><i><em class="italic" style="white-space: pre-wrap;">Figure 25.</em></i> <i><em class="italic" style="white-space: pre-wrap;">Code for request line injection.</em></i></figcaption></figure><figure class="kg-card kg-image-card kg-card-hascaption"><img src="https://blog.talosintelligence.com/content/images/2026/02/data-src-image-4d5dacab-9cda-4732-ba60-3618cfc05a9f.png" class="kg-image" alt="" loading="lazy" width="480" height="693"><figcaption><i><em class="italic" style="white-space: pre-wrap;">Figure 26.</em></i> <i><em class="italic" style="white-space: pre-wrap;">Part of</em></i> <i><em class="italic" style="white-space: pre-wrap;">HAProxy</em></i> <i><em class="italic" style="white-space: pre-wrap;">configuration.</em></i></figcaption></figure><h3 id="yitijibin">Yitiji.bin&nbsp;</h3><p><code>Yitiji.bin</code>&nbsp;is a&nbsp;DKnife&nbsp;component&nbsp;that creates a bridged TAP interface on the router to host and route attacker-injected LAN traffic. It creates a virtual TAP interface named&nbsp;“yitiji”,&nbsp;using the IP address&nbsp;<code>10.3.3.3</code>&nbsp;and MAC address<em>&nbsp;<code>1E:17:8E:C6:56:40</code></em>, and bridges that interface to the real network.&nbsp;</p><p>DKnife&nbsp;responds to binary download requests using URL points to the&nbsp;Yitiji&nbsp;interface (e.g.,&nbsp;<code>http://10.3.3.3:81/app/base.apk</code>). When such a request is received, the&nbsp;<code>dknife.bin</code>&nbsp;component&nbsp;forwards&nbsp;the traffic to UDP port 555, where&nbsp;<code>yitiji.bin</code>&nbsp;is listening. The&nbsp;component&nbsp;then&nbsp;determines&nbsp;the&nbsp;appropriate link-layer&nbsp;encapsulation, reconstructs complete Ethernet/IP/TCP frames (primarily TCP and ICMP), corrects packet lengths and checksums, and injects them into the TAP interface. This causes the kernel to treat the forged traffic as legitimate LAN communication. Through this mechanism,&nbsp;DKnife&nbsp;can receive the binary download request and serve the payload via this interface. In the reverse direction,&nbsp;Yitiji&nbsp;captures packets leaving the TAP, restores their original VLAN/PPPoE/4G headers, recalculates IP and TCP checksums, and transmits them through the physical network interface specified in the configuration file<em>&nbsp;<code>/dksoft/conf/wxha.conf</code></em>. It also fabricates ARP&nbsp;replies&nbsp;so other hosts treat the interface as a device in the LAN.&nbsp;</p><p>In this way,&nbsp;Yitiji&nbsp;creates&nbsp;a distinct LAN for delivering the malware. This&nbsp;approach&nbsp;facilitates&nbsp;the&nbsp;AitM&nbsp;attack for binary downloads in a stealthy way that avoids IP conflicts and&nbsp;detection.&nbsp;&nbsp;</p><h3 id="remotebin">Remote.bin&nbsp;</h3><p>This&nbsp;component&nbsp;functions as&nbsp;an&nbsp;<a href="https://github.com/ntop/n2n" rel="noreferrer noopener"><u>N2N</u></a>&nbsp;peer-to-peer VPN client. When executed it creates a virtual network device named&nbsp;“edge0”&nbsp;and attaches it to a P2P overlay, automatically joining the hardcoded community&nbsp;<code>dknife</code>&nbsp;and registering with the embedded&nbsp;supernode. All traffic routed into edge0 is encapsulated and&nbsp;forwarded&nbsp;over UDP to overlay peers, and the binary also binds a management UDP port on 5644.&nbsp;</p><p>With this&nbsp;component, the gateway itself becomes reachable from the overlay and can serve as an egress point for data exfiltration. The implementation supports&nbsp;Twofish&nbsp;encryption if an N2N_KEY environment variable is supplied, but no such key was embedded in the&nbsp;analysed&nbsp;code or associated files.&nbsp;</p><h3 id="mmdownbin">Mmdown.bin&nbsp;</h3><p>This binary is a simple Android APK malware downloader and update&nbsp;component&nbsp;in the&nbsp;DKnife&nbsp;framework. It communicates with a hardcoded C2 (<code>http://47.93.54[.]134:8005</code>) and periodically checks for an update manifest and then downloads whatever files the server specifies.&nbsp;</p><p>On startup it ensures a handful of&nbsp;local directories exist and generates or reads the UUID from file&nbsp;/etc/diankeuuid&nbsp;to uses it as the filename for the downloaded per-host manifest file&nbsp;<code>&lt;UUID&gt;.mm</code>.&nbsp;The&nbsp;“.mm”&nbsp;file is a list of URLs and MD5 pairs in the format of&nbsp;<code>http://[URL]&lt;TAB&gt;&lt;16-byte MD5&gt;</code>. After downloading the manifest file, it parses the file and repeatedly&nbsp;attempts&nbsp;to download each URL over plain HTTP, verifies the downloaded file’s MD5, and on success copies the file into the local web content directory&nbsp;<code>/dksoft/html/app/</code>. When one or more files are successfully fetched it archives the manifest into&nbsp;<code>/dksoft/conf/&lt;UUID&gt;.mm</code>&nbsp;and updates internal&nbsp;MD5 bookkeeping so it&nbsp;doesn’t&nbsp;repeatedly download the same files.&nbsp;</p><h3 id="dkupdatebin">Dkupdate.bin&nbsp;</h3><p>This binary functions as a&nbsp;DKnife&nbsp;download, deploy, and update&nbsp;component&nbsp;similar to&nbsp;the downloader we initially discovered, but with&nbsp;additional&nbsp;capabilities. It retrieves an update archive <code>update_bin.tar.gz</code> from a&nbsp;C2&nbsp;server (using a different embedded default URL:&nbsp;<code>http://117.175.185[.]81:8003/</code>), launches a separate binary called&nbsp;<code>eth5to2.bin</code>&nbsp;(not included in the downloaded archive, likely for traffic forwarding) and starts Nginx to run the web server to serve the hijacking components that manipulate HTTP/HTTPS responses.</p><h3 id="getting-network-devices-information">Getting Network Devices Information&nbsp;</h3><p>In both&nbsp;<code>dknife.bin</code>&nbsp;and&nbsp;<code>postapi.bin</code>&nbsp;components,&nbsp;DKnife&nbsp;tries to login to an interface which is likely for router management at&nbsp;<code>192.168.92.92:8080</code>&nbsp;via the following POST request to retrieve network users and PPPOE information. The POST request for login and getting device information both sent a password MD5 (which is the MD5&nbsp;of&nbsp;<code>q1w2e3r4</code>) for authentication. If successful login, the server replies with a device serial number (SN) and number of users currently registered. If the number is not zero, the implant requests for the list of&nbsp;MAC&nbsp;and&nbsp;PPPoE&nbsp;ID mapping.&nbsp;</p>
<!--kg-card-begin: html-->
<pre>POST /login HTTP/1.1 

Host: 192.168.92.92:8080 

Content-Type: application/json 

Content-Length: 38 

 

{"passwdMD5":"c62d929e7b7e7b6165923a5dfc60cb56"} 

 

POST /fe-device-info HTTP/1.1 

Host: 192.168.92.92:8080 

User-Agent: Mozilla/5.0 

Cookie: feWebSession={"sessionId":**<g_session_id>**} 

Content-Length: 48 

 

{"passwdMD5":"c62d929e7b7e7b6165923a5dfc60cb56"} 

 

POST /user HTTP/1.1 

Host: 192.168.92.92:8080 

User-Agent: Mozilla/5.0 

Cookie: feWebSession={"sessionId":<g_session_id>} 

Content-Type: application/json 

Content-Length: 15 

 

{"index":"all"} </pre>

<figure class="kg-card kg-image-card kg-card-hascaption"><img src="https://blog.talosintelligence.com/content/images/2026/02/data-src-image-66c075ec-4fc8-442e-b226-5f44eccb3825.png" class="kg-image" alt="" loading="lazy" width="652" height="418" srcset="https://blog.talosintelligence.com/content/images/size/w600/2026/02/data-src-image-66c075ec-4fc8-442e-b226-5f44eccb3825.png 600w, https://blog.talosintelligence.com/content/images/2026/02/data-src-image-66c075ec-4fc8-442e-b226-5f44eccb3825.png 652w"><figcaption><i><em class="italic" style="white-space: pre-wrap;">Figure</em></i> <i><em class="italic" style="white-space: pre-wrap;">27.</em></i> <i><em class="italic" style="white-space: pre-wrap;">Code parsing the session ID response from management interface.</em></i></figcaption></figure><h2 id="conclusion">Conclusion&nbsp;</h2><p>Routers and edge devices&nbsp;remain&nbsp;prime targets in sophisticated targeted attack campaigns. As threat actors intensify their efforts to compromise this infrastructure, understanding the tools and TTPs they employ is critical. The discovery of the&nbsp;DKnife&nbsp;framework highlights the advanced capabilities of modern&nbsp;AitM&nbsp;threats, which blend deep‑packet inspection, traffic manipulation, and customized malware delivery across a wide range of device types. Overall, the evidence suggests a well‑integrated and evolving toolchain of&nbsp;AitM&nbsp;frameworks and backdoors, underscoring the need for continuous visibility and monitoring of routers and edge infrastructure.&nbsp;</p><h2 id="appendix">Appendix&nbsp;</h2><h3 id="configuration-files">Configuration Files&nbsp;</h3>

<h2 id="coverage">Coverage&nbsp;</h2><p>The following&nbsp;ClamAV&nbsp;signature detects and blocks this threat:&nbsp;</p><ul><li>Win.Trojan.Shadowpad-10010830-1&nbsp;</li><li>Win.Loader.WizardNet-10044819-0&nbsp;</li><li>Win.Trojan.DarkNimbus-10059255-0&nbsp;&nbsp;</li><li>Win.Trojan.DKnife-10059257-0&nbsp;</li><li>Unix.Trojan.DKnife-10059259-0 &nbsp;&nbsp;</li><li>Win.Trojan.DKnife-10059260-0 &nbsp;&nbsp;</li></ul><p>The following Snort rules cover this&nbsp;threat:&nbsp;</p><ul><li>Snort 2 – 65533</li><li>Snort 3 – 65533</li></ul><h2 id="indicators-of-compromise-iocs">Indicators of Compromise (IoCs)&nbsp;</h2><p>IOCs for this research can also be found at our GitHub repository&nbsp;<a href="https://github.com/Cisco-Talos/IOCs/blob/main/2026/02/knife-cutting-the-edge.txt" rel="noreferrer">here</a>.</p>

    <script>
  function coverageView() {
    const coverageTable = document.getElementById('coverage-block');
    const sidebar = document.getElementById('side-bar');
    const postContent = document.getElementById('post-content');
    const currentWidth = window.innerWidth;  
  
    if (coverageTable && sidebar && postContent) {
      if (currentWidth < 1000) {
        // For viewport less than 1000px, append to post content
        postContent.appendChild(coverageTable);
      } else {
        // For viewport 1000px and wider, check for subscribe button
        const subscribeButton = sidebar.querySelector('.subscribe-button');
        
        if (subscribeButton) {
          // Insert coverage block after the subscribe button
          subscribeButton.insertAdjacentElement('afterend', coverageTable);
        } else {
          // No subscribe button found, prepend to top of sidebar
          sidebar.prepend(coverageTable);
        }
      }
    }
  }
  
  window.addEventListener("resize", coverageView);
  window.addEventListener("load", coverageView);
</script>
